   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading">Emoji</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>

     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Symbols used as emotional cues in text
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Emoticon" title="Emoticon">Emoticon</a>.
      </div>

      <p><b>Emoji</b> or <b>Emojis</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/?/: 'i' in 'kit'">?</span><span title="/?/: primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/o?/: 'o' in 'code'">o?</span><span title="/d?/: 'j' in 'jam'">d?</span><span title="/i?/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">i?</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">?-<span style="font-size:90%">MOH</span>-jee</i></a>; from <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> ??? <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Japanese" title="Help:IPA/Japanese">[emod?i]</a></span> <a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a><span style="white-space:nowrap">?</span>'picture character'; plural <i>emoji</i> or <i>emojis</i><sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>) are <a href="/wiki/Pictogram" title="Pictogram">pictograms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Logogram" title="Logogram">logograms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ideogram" title="Ideogram">ideograms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smiley" title="Smiley">smileys</a> used in electronic messages and <a href="/wiki/Web_page" title="Web page">web pages</a>. The emoji's primary function is to fill in emotional cues otherwise missing from typed conversation.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Some examples of emojis are ?, ?????, ?, ?, ?, ?, ?, ??, ?, and ?. Emojis exist in various genres, including facial expressions, common objects, places and types of weather, and animals. They are much like <a href="/wiki/Emoticon" title="Emoticon">emoticons</a>, but emojis are pictures rather than <a href="/wiki/Typographic_approximation" title="Typographic approximation">typographic approximations</a>; the term "emoji" in the strict sense refers to such pictures which can be represented as <a href="/wiki/Character_encoding" title="Character encoding">encoded characters</a>, but it is sometimes applied to <a href="/wiki/Sticker_(messaging)" title="Sticker (messaging)">messaging stickers</a> by extension.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Originally meaning <a href="/wiki/Pictograph" class="mw-redirect" title="Pictograph">pictograph</a>, the word <i>emoji</i> comes from Japanese <i lang="ja-Latn" title="Hepburn transliteration"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%B5%B5" class="extiw" title="wikt:?">e</a></i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese-language text">?</span>, "picture")</span> + <i lang="ja-Latn" title="Hepburn transliteration"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%96%87%E5%AD%97" class="extiw" title="wikt:??">moji</a></i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese-language text">??</span>, "character")</span>; the resemblance to the English words <i>emotion</i> and <i>emoticon</i> is <a href="/wiki/False_cognate" title="False cognate">purely coincidental</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/ISO_15924" title="ISO 15924">ISO 15924</a> script code for emoji is <code>Zsye</code>. </p>
      <p>Originating on <a href="/wiki/Japanese_mobile_phone_culture" title="Japanese mobile phone culture">Japanese mobile phones</a> in 1997, emojis became increasingly popular worldwide in the 2010s after being added to several mobile operating systems.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> They are now considered to be a large part of <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">popular culture</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_World" class="mw-redirect" title="Western World">the West</a> and around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In 2015, <a href="/wiki/OxfordDictionaries.com" class="mw-redirect" title="OxfordDictionaries.com">Oxford Dictionaries</a> named the <a href="/wiki/Face_with_Tears_of_Joy_emoji" title="Face with Tears of Joy emoji">Face with Tears of Joy emoji</a> (?) the <a href="/wiki/Word_of_the_year#Oxford" title="Word of the year">Word of the Year (UK)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> </p>

           <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Technical_aspects"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Technical aspects</span></a> 
            <ul> 
             <li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-16"><a href="#Supplementary_Multilingual_Plane_support"><span class="tocnumber">7.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Supplementary Multilingual Plane support</span></a></li>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
      <h3><span id="The_origin_of_emoji_pictograms_.281990s.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_origin_of_emoji_pictograms_(1990s)">The origin of emoji pictograms (1990s)</span></h3> 
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Unicode character block
      </div>

      <p>The emoji was predated by the <a href="/wiki/Emoticon" title="Emoticon">emoticon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> a basic text-based version of the now established <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> emoji language and likely took inspiration from <a href="/wiki/Pictogram" title="Pictogram">pictograms</a>. Numerous attempts in the 1990s were made in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> to enhance the basic emoticon to make it more desirable for use.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The emoji is based on the premise of using text markers to form images. This dates back to the 1960s, when <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> novelist and professor <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Nabokov" title="Vladimir Nabokov">Vladimir Nabokov</a> stated in an interview with <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> <i>"I often think there should exist a special typographical sign for a smile — some sort of concave mark, a supine round bracket."</i><sup id="cite_ref-Nabokov1973_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nabokov1973-18">[18]</a></sup> However, it wasn't until the 1980s when computer scientist <a href="/wiki/Scott_Fahlman" title="Scott Fahlman">Scott Fahlman</a> invented the emoticon, by suggesting that&nbsp;:-) and&nbsp;:-( could replace language.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the early 1990s, there were a number of digital <a href="/wiki/Smiley" title="Smiley">smileys</a> and emoticons that were used in fonts, pictograms, graphical representations, and even welcome messages. The font <a href="/wiki/Wingdings" title="Wingdings">Wingdings</a>, designed and used on <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> platforms, included pictographs such as smiley and sad faces, and first appeared on <a href="/wiki/Windows" class="mw-redirect" title="Windows">Windows</a> and other Microsoft platforms from 1990 onwards. In late 1995, it was announced in the French newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Monde" title="Le Monde">Le Monde</a></i> that telecoms company <a href="/wiki/Alcatel-Lucent" title="Alcatel-Lucent">Alcatel</a> would be launching a mobile phone to be released in 1996. The newspaper article displays the BC 600, with the welcome screen displaying a digital smiley face.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Versions of the <a href="/wiki/Nokia" title="Nokia">Nokia</a> phone also contained sets of graphics, which in 2001 they were still referring to as smileys.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Although Wingdings and <a href="/wiki/Webdings" title="Webdings">Webdings</a>, as custom-encoded <a href="/wiki/Pi_font" class="mw-redirect" title="Pi font">pi fonts</a>, could be used to send pictographs in <a href="/wiki/Rich_text" class="mw-redirect" title="Rich text">rich text</a> messages to platforms providing those fonts, they would appear as letters or other symbols where this was not supported. For example, a <a href="/wiki/National_park" title="National park">national park</a> pictogram (?) was available in Webdings at <a href="/wiki/Hexadecimal" title="Hexadecimal">0x</a>50, which corresponded to the capital letter <a href="/wiki/P_(letter)" class="mw-redirect" title="P (letter)">P</a> encoded in <a href="/wiki/ASCII" title="ASCII">ASCII</a>. In the late 1990s, <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone" title="Mobile phone">mobile phone</a> carriers in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> implemented emoji sets for use on their platforms; these Japanese cellular emoji differed from pi fonts in supporting both pictographs and regular text in a single <a href="/wiki/Character_encoding" title="Character encoding">character encoding</a> system, allowing them to be freely mixed in plain text messages.<sup id="cite_ref-emojipediafirst_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emojipediafirst-21">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Emojipedia" title="Emojipedia">Emojipedia</a> released findings in early 2019 stating they believed the SkyWalker DP-211SW, a mobile telephone manufactured by J-Phone which supported a set of 90 emoji, to be the first phone known to contain a set of emojis as part of its typeface, dating it back to 1997. These included emoji which remain popular today, such as the <a href="/wiki/Pile_of_Poo_emoji" title="Pile of Poo emoji">Pile of Poo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-emojipediafirst_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emojipediafirst-21">[21]</a></sup> The J-Phone DP-211SW didn't sell well due to its high retail price, and therefore mass-market adoption of emoji didn't take place at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> J-Phone later became <a href="/wiki/Vodafone#Japan" title="Vodafone">Vodafone Japan</a> and is now <a href="/wiki/SoftBank_Mobile" class="mw-redirect" title="SoftBank Mobile">SoftBank Mobile</a>; a later, expanded version of the SoftBank emoji set was the basis for the emoji selection available on early <a href="/wiki/IPhone" title="IPhone">iPhones</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-emojipediafirst_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emojipediafirst-21">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A highly influential early set of 176 cellular emoji was created by <a href="/wiki/Shigetaka_Kurita" title="Shigetaka Kurita">Shigetaka Kurita</a> in 1999,<sup id="cite_ref-Steinmetz2015_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinmetz2015-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and deployed on <a href="/wiki/NTT_DoCoMo" class="mw-redirect" title="NTT DoCoMo">NTT DoCoMo</a>'s <a href="/wiki/I-mode" title="I-mode">i-mode</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Mobile_web" title="Mobile web">Mobile web</a> platform.<sup id="cite_ref-Meet_Shigetaka_Kurita,_the_Father_of_Emoji_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meet_Shigetaka_Kurita,_the_Father_of_Emoji-25">[25]</a></sup> They were intended to help facilitate electronic communication, and to serve as a distinguishing feature from other services.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5">[5]</a></sup> Due to their influence, Kurita's designs were once frequently claimed to be the first cellular emoji;<sup id="cite_ref-emojipediafirst_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emojipediafirst-21">[21]</a></sup> however, Kurita has denied this to be the case.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> According to interviews, he took inspiration from Japanese <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a> where characters are often drawn with symbolic representations called <i>manpu</i> (such as a water drop on a face representing representing nervousness or confusion), and weather pictograms used to depict the weather conditions at any given time. He also drew inspiration from <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a> and street sign pictograms.<sup id="cite_ref-Meet_Shigetaka_Kurita,_the_Father_of_Emoji_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meet_Shigetaka_Kurita,_the_Father_of_Emoji-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Why_and_how_I_created_emoji_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Why_and_how_I_created_emoji-29">[29]</a></sup> These emoji represent a Japanese visual style commonly found in manga and <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a>, combined with <i><a href="/wiki/Kaomoji" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaomoji">kaomoji</a></i> and smiley elements.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Kurita's work is now displayed in the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="Museum of Modern Art">Museum of Modern Art</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> An additional 76 emoji, besides the 176 basic emoji, were added in phones supporting <a href="/wiki/C-HTML" class="mw-redirect" title="C-HTML">C-HTML</a> 4.0.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The first set of J-Phone emoji were in black and white, while later revisions introduced multicolor glyphs, whereas Kurita's were brightly colored, albeit with a single color per glyph. Both sets were made up of generic images that depicted numbers, sports, the time, moon phases and the weather. Both Kurita's and SoftBank's designs were 12×12-pixel emoji pictograms.<sup id="cite_ref-emojipediafirst_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emojipediafirst-21">[21]</a></sup> A third notable emoji set was introduced by Japanese mobile phone brand <a href="/wiki/Au_by_KDDI" class="mw-redirect" title="Au by KDDI">au by KDDI</a>, which was influential on early <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> emoji designs.<sup id="cite_ref-emojipediafirst_21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emojipediafirst-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-schwartzberg_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schwartzberg-33">[33]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Various, often incompatible, character encoding schemes were developed by the different mobile providers in Japan for their own emoji sets. When transmitted in <a href="/wiki/Shift_JIS" title="Shift JIS">Shift JIS</a> on NTT DoCoMo, emoji symbols are specified as a two-<a href="/wiki/Byte" title="Byte">byte</a> sequence in the range F89F through F9FC (as expressed in <a href="/wiki/Hexadecimal" title="Hexadecimal">hexadecimal</a>). Emoji pictograms on au by KDDI are specified using the <a href="/wiki/HTML_element#Images_and_objects" title="HTML element">IMG tag</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> encoded in Shift JIS between F340 and F7FC,<sup id="cite_ref-EmojiSources_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EmojiSources-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-utcL210132_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utcL210132-35">[35]</a></sup> or encoded in extended <a href="/wiki/JIS_X_0208" title="JIS X 0208">JIS X 0208</a> between 7521 and 7B73.<sup id="cite_ref-utcL210132_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utcL210132-35">[35]</a></sup> SoftBank Mobile emoji support colors and animation, and use different formats on 2G versus 3G:<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> in the 2G format, they are encoded in sequences using the <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r936282034">.mw-parser-output .control-code-link-internal{border-bottom:1px dashed #86a1ff}.mw-parser-output .control-code-link-internal a{color:inherit}</style><a href="/wiki/C0_and_C1_control_codes#ESC" title="C0 and C1 control codes">Escape</a> and 
       <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r936282034"><a href="/wiki/Shift_In" class="mw-redirect" title="Shift In">Shift In</a> control characters, whereas in the 3G format, they are encoded in Shift JIS between F741 and FBDE.<sup id="cite_ref-EmojiSources_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EmojiSources-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-utcL210132_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utcL210132-35">[35]</a></sup> The SoftBank 3G format collides with the overlapping ranges used by the other vendors: for example, the Shift JIS representation F797 is used for a <a href="/wiki/Convenience_store" title="Convenience store">convenience store</a> (?) by SoftBank, but for a <a href="/wiki/Wristwatch" class="mw-redirect" title="Wristwatch">wristwatch</a> (??) by KDDI.<sup id="cite_ref-EmojiSources_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EmojiSources-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-utcL210132_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utcL210132-35">[35]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>DoCoMo<sup id="cite_ref-utcL210132_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utcL210132-35">[35]</a></sup> and SoftBank<sup id="cite_ref-gmoji_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gmoji-37">[37]</a></sup> also developed their own schemes for representing their emoji sets in extended JIS X 0208 between 7522 and 7E38. These often matched the encodings of similar KDDI emoji where they existed: for example, the camera (?) was represented in Shift JIS as F8E2 by DoCoMo, F6EE by KDDI, and F948 by SoftBank, but as 7670 in JIS by all three.<sup id="cite_ref-utcL210132_35-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utcL210132-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gmoji_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gmoji-37">[37]</a></sup> All three vendors also developed schemes for encoding their emoji in the Unicode <a href="/wiki/Private_Use_Area" class="mw-redirect" title="Private Use Area">Private Use Area</a>: DoCoMo, for example, used the range U+E63E through U+E757.<sup id="cite_ref-utcL210132_35-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utcL210132-35">[35]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Development_of_emoji_sets_.282000.E2.80.932009.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_emoji_sets_(2000–2009)">Development of emoji sets (2000–2009)</span></h3> 
      <p>The basic 12×12 pixel emoji in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> grew in popularity across various platforms over the next decade. This was aided by the popularity of DoCoMo i-mode, which for many was the origins of the <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphone</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2020)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The i-mode service also saw the introduction of emojis in conversation form on messenger <a href="/wiki/Application_software" title="Application software">apps</a>. By 2004, i-mode had 40 million subscribers, meaning numerous people were exposed to the emoji for the first time between 2000 and 2004. The popularity of i-mode led to other manufacturers competing with similar offerings and therefore developed their own emoji sets. While emoji adoption was high in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> during this time, the companies failed to collaborate and come up with a uniform set of emojis to be used across all platforms in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Universal_Coded_Character_Set" title="Universal Coded Character Set">Universal Coded Character Set</a> (<a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a>), overseen by the <a href="/wiki/Unicode_Consortium" title="Unicode Consortium">Unicode Consortium</a> and <a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_JTC_1/SC_2" title="ISO/IEC JTC 1/SC 2">ISO/IEC JTC 1/SC 2</a>, had already been established as the international standard for text representation (<a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_10646" class="mw-redirect" title="ISO/IEC 10646">ISO/IEC 10646</a>) since 1993, although variants of <a href="/wiki/Shift_JIS" title="Shift JIS">Shift JIS</a> remained relatively common in Japan. Unicode included several characters which would subsequently be classified as emoji, including some from <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North American</a> or <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western European</a> sources such as <a href="/wiki/Code_page_437" title="Code page 437">DOS code page 437</a>, <a href="/wiki/ITC_Zapf_Dingbats" class="mw-redirect" title="ITC Zapf Dingbats">ITC Zapf Dingbats</a> or the <a href="/wiki/WordPerfect" title="WordPerfect">WordPerfect</a> Iconic Symbols set.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Unicode's coverage of written characters was extended several times by new editions during the 2000s, with little interest in incorporating the Japanese cellular emoji sets (which were deemed out of scope),<sup id="cite_ref-principles_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-principles-41">[41]</a></sup> although symbol characters which would subsequently be classified as emoji continued to be added. For example, Unicode 4.0 release contained 16 new emojis, which included direction arrows, a warning triangle, and an eject button.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Besides Zapf Dingbats, other <a href="/wiki/Dingbat" title="Dingbat">dingbat</a> fonts such as Wingdings or Webdings also included additional pictographic symbols in their own custom pi font encodings; unlike Zapf Dingbats, however, many of these would not be available as Unicode emoji until 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-hexuswebdings_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hexuswebdings-43">[43]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/The_Smiley_Company" title="The Smiley Company">The Smiley Company</a> developed The Smiley Dictionary, which was launched in 2001. The desktop platform was aimed at allowing people to insert smileys as text when sending emails and writing on a <a href="/wiki/Desktop_computer" title="Desktop computer">desktop computer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-eveningstandard_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eveningstandard-44">[44]</a></sup> The smiley toolbar offered a variety of symbols and smileys and was used on platforms such as <a href="/wiki/MSN_Messenger" class="mw-redirect" title="MSN Messenger">MSN Messenger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nokia" title="Nokia">Nokia</a> as one of the largest telecoms companies globally at the time, were still referring to today's emoji sets as <a href="/wiki/Smiley" title="Smiley">smileys</a> in 2001.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The digital smiley movement was headed up by Nicolas Loufrani, the CEO of The Smiley Company.<sup id="cite_ref-eveningstandard_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eveningstandard-44">[44]</a></sup> He created a smiley toolbar, which was available at smileydictionary.com during the early 2000s to be sent as emojis are today.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Mobile providers in both the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> began discussions on how to introduce their own emoji sets from 2004 onwards. It wasn't until <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> employees requested that <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> look into the possibility of a uniform emoji set did many companies begin to take the emoji seriously. <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a> quickly followed and began to collaborate with not only <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>, but also providers in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>. In August 2007, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Davis_(Unicode)" title="Mark Davis (Unicode)">Mark Davis</a> and his colleagues Kat Momoi and Markus Scherer wrote the first draft for consideration by the Unicode Technical Committee (UTC) to introduce emojis into the <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode standard</a>. The UTC, having previously deemed emoji to be out of scope for Unicode, made the decision to broaden this scope, to enable compatibility with the Japanese cellular carrier formats which were becoming more widespread.<sup id="cite_ref-principles_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-principles-41">[41]</a></sup> Peter Edberg and Yasuo Kida joined the collaborative efforts from <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple Inc.</a> shortly after and the official UTC proposal as co-authors came in January 2009. </p>
      <p>Separately, a proposal had been submitted in 2008 to add the <a href="/wiki/ARIB_STD_B24_character_set" title="ARIB STD B24 character set">ARIB extended characters</a> used in broadcasting in Japan to Unicode. This included several pictographic symbols.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> These were added in Unicode 5.2 in 2009, a year before the cellular emoji sets were fully added; they include several characters which either also appeared amongst the cellular emoji<sup id="cite_ref-utcL210132_35-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utcL210132-35">[35]</a></sup> or were subsequently classified as emoji.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Throughout 2009, members of the <a href="/wiki/Unicode_Consortium" title="Unicode Consortium">Unicode Consortium</a> and national standardization bodies of various countries gave feedback and proposed changes to the international standardization of the emoji. The feedback from various bodies in the United States, Europe, and Japan agreed on a set of 722 emojis as the standard set, which would be released in 2010 as Unicode 6.0.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Modern-day_emojis_.282010.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern-day_emojis_(2010–present)">Modern-day emojis (2010–present)</span></h3> 
      <p>The popularity of emoji has caused pressure from vendors and international markets to add additional designs into the Unicode standard to meet the demands of different cultures. Unicode 7.0 added approximately 250 emoji, many from the <a href="/wiki/Webdings" title="Webdings">Webdings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wingdings" title="Wingdings">Wingdings</a> fonts.<sup id="cite_ref-hexuswebdings_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hexuswebdings-43">[43]</a></sup> Some characters now defined as emoji are inherited from a variety of pre-Unicode messenger systems not only used in Japan, including <a href="/wiki/Yahoo_Messenger" class="mw-redirect" title="Yahoo Messenger">Yahoo</a> and <a href="/wiki/MSN_Messenger" class="mw-redirect" title="MSN Messenger">MSN Messenger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Emoji_Additions:_Animals,_Compatibility,_and_More_Popular_Requests;_Emoji_tranche_5_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emoji_Additions:_Animals,_Compatibility,_and_More_Popular_Requests;_Emoji_tranche_5-50">[50]</a></sup> Unicode 8.0 added another 41 emoji, including articles of sports equipment such as the cricket bat, food items such as the <a href="/wiki/Taco" title="Taco">taco</a>, signs of the <a href="/wiki/Zodiac" title="Zodiac">Zodiac</a>, new facial expressions, and symbols for places of worship.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There are several sources of emoji characters. A single character can exist in multiple sources, and characters from a source were unified with existing characters where appropriate: for example, the "shower" weather symbol (??) from the ARIB source was unified with an existing umbrella with raindrops character,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> which had been added for <a href="/wiki/KPS_9566" title="KPS 9566">KPS 9566</a> compatibility.<sup id="cite_ref-utc-L2-02-102_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utc-L2-02-102-53">[53]</a></sup> The emoji characters named "Rain"<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese-language text">"?"</span>, <i lang="ja-Latn" title="Hepburn transliteration">ame</i>)</span> from all three Japanese carriers were in turn unified with the ARIB character.<sup id="cite_ref-utcL210132_35-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utcL210132-35">[35]</a></sup> However, the Unicode Consortium groups the most significant sources of emoji into four categories:<sup id="cite_ref-UTR51_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UTR51-54">[54]</a></sup> </p> 
      <table class="wikitable"> 
       <tbody>

       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>Corporate demand for emoji standardization has placed pressures on the Unicode Consortium, with some members complaining that it had overtaken the group's traditional focus on standardizing characters used for minority languages and transcribing historical records.<sup id="cite_ref-Inside_'Emojigeddon'_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Inside_'Emojigeddon'-59">[58]</a></sup> Conversely, the Consortium recognises that public desire for emoji support has put pressure on vendors to improve their Unicode support,<sup id="cite_ref-detract_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-detract-60">[59]</a></sup> which is especially true for characters outside the <a href="/wiki/Basic_Multilingual_Plane" class="mw-redirect" title="Basic Multilingual Plane">Basic Multilingual Plane</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-backendless_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-backendless-61">[60]</a></sup> thus leading to better support for Unicode's historic and minority scripts in deployed software.<sup id="cite_ref-detract_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-detract-60">[59]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Emoji characters vary slightly between platforms within the limits in meaning defined by the Unicode specification, as companies have tried to provide artistic presentations of ideas and objects.<sup id="cite_ref-Lost_in_translation:_Android_emoji_vs_iOS_emoji_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lost_in_translation:_Android_emoji_vs_iOS_emoji-62">[61]</a></sup> For example, following an Apple tradition, the calendar emoji on Apple products always shows July 17, the date in 2002 Apple announced its <a href="/wiki/ICal" class="mw-redirect" title="ICal">iCal</a> calendar application for <a href="/wiki/MacOS" title="MacOS">macOS</a>. This led some Apple product users to initially nickname July 17 "<a href="/wiki/World_Emoji_Day" title="World Emoji Day">World Emoji Day</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> Other emoji fonts show different dates or do not show a specific one.<sup id="cite_ref-Calendar_emoji_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calendar_emoji-64">[63]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Some Apple emoji are very similar to the SoftBank standard, since SoftBank was the first Japanese network on which the iPhone launched. For example, <span class="nowrap"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886049734">.mw-parser-output .monospaced{font-family:monospace,monospace}</style><span class="monospaced">U+1F483</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <a href="/wiki/%F0%9F%92%83" class="mw-redirect" title="?"><span style="font-size:85%;">DANCER</span></a> is female on Apple and SoftBank standards but male or gender-neutral on others.<sup id="cite_ref-How_Emoji_Get_Lost_In_Translation_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_Emoji_Get_Lost_In_Translation-65">[64]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Journalists have noted that the ambiguity of emoji has allowed them to take on culture-specific meanings not present in the original <a href="/wiki/Glyph" title="Glyph">glyphs</a>. For example, <span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F485</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <a href="/wiki/%F0%9F%92%85" class="mw-redirect" title="?"><span style="font-size:85%;">NAIL POLISH</span></a> has been described as being used in English-language communities to signify "non-caring fabulousness"<sup id="cite_ref-How_to_(pretend_to)_be_young_and_down_with_the_internet_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_to_(pretend_to)_be_young_and_down_with_the_internet-66">[65]</a></sup> and "anything from shutting <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/hater" class="extiw" title="wikt:hater">haters</a> down to a sense of accomplishment".<sup id="cite_ref-The_31_Most_Nail_Care_Emoji_Moments_Of_2014_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_31_Most_Nail_Care_Emoji_Moments_Of_2014-67">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Five_Non-Negotiable_Best_Emojis_in_the_Land_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Five_Non-Negotiable_Best_Emojis_in_the_Land-68">[67]</a></sup> Unicode manuals sometimes provide notes on auxiliary meanings of an object to guide designers on how emoji may be used, for example noting that some users may expect <span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F4BA</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;">SEAT</span> to stand for "a reserved or ticketed seat, as for an airplane, train, or theater".<sup id="cite_ref-Unicode_Consortium_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Unicode_Consortium-69">[68]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As of July&nbsp;2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Emoji&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there were 2,666 emoji on the official Unicode Standard list.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_influence">Cultural influence</span></h3> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">
      <div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;max-width:162px">

         <div class="tsingle" style="width:52px;max-width:52px">

          Color illustrations of <span class="nowrap">
           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F602</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <a href="/wiki/Face_with_Tears_of_Joy_emoji" title="Face with Tears of Joy emoji"><span style="font-size:85%;">FACE WITH TEARS OF JOY</span></a> from <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Noto_Emoji_Project" class="mw-redirect" title="Noto Emoji Project">Noto Emoji Project</a> and <a href="/wiki/Firefox_OS" title="Firefox OS">Firefox OS</a>
         </div>

      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Oxford_Dictionaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford Dictionaries">Oxford Dictionaries</a> named <span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F602</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <a href="/wiki/Face_with_Tears_of_Joy_emoji" title="Face with Tears of Joy emoji"><span style="font-size:85%;">FACE WITH TEARS OF JOY</span></a><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> its 2015 <a href="/wiki/Word_of_the_year#Oxford" title="Word of the year">Word of the Year</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford2015_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford2015-72">[71]</a></sup> Oxford noted that 2015 had seen a sizable increase in the use of the word "emoji" and recognized its impact on popular culture.<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford2015_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford2015-72">[71]</a></sup> Oxford Dictionaries President Caspar Grathwohl expressed that "traditional alphabet scripts have been struggling to meet the rapid-fire, visually focused demands of 21st Century communication. It's not surprising that a pictographic script like emoji has stepped in to fill those gaps—it's flexible, immediate, and infuses tone beautifully."<sup id="cite_ref-Waldman2015_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waldman2015-73">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/SwiftKey" class="mw-redirect" title="SwiftKey">SwiftKey</a> found that "Face with Tears of Joy" was the most popular emoji across the world.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Dialect_Society" title="American Dialect Society">American Dialect Society</a> declared <span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F346</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;">AUBERGINE</span> to be the "Most Notable Emoji" of 2015 in their Word of the Year vote.<sup id="cite_ref-AmericanDialect_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmericanDialect-75">[74]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Some emoji are specific to Japanese culture, such as a <a href="/wiki/Dogeza" title="Dogeza">bowing</a> businessman (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F647</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>), the <a href="/wiki/Shoshinsha_mark" title="Shoshinsha mark">shoshinsha mark</a> used to indicate a beginner driver (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F530</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>), a white flower (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F4AE</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>) used to denote "brilliant homework",<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> or a group of emoji representing popular foods: <a href="/wiki/Ramen" title="Ramen">ramen</a> noodles (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F35C</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>), <a href="/wiki/Dango" title="Dango">dango</a> (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F361</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>), <a href="/wiki/Onigiri" title="Onigiri">onigiri</a> (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F359</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>), <a href="/wiki/Japanese_curry" title="Japanese curry">Japanese curry</a> (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F35B</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>), and <a href="/wiki/Sushi" title="Sushi">sushi</a> (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F363</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>). <a href="/wiki/Unicode_Consortium" title="Unicode Consortium">Unicode Consortium</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Mark_Davis_(Unicode)" title="Mark Davis (Unicode)">Mark Davis</a> compared the use of emoji to a developing language, particularly mentioning the American use of eggplant (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F346</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>) to represent a <a href="/wiki/Phallus" title="Phallus">phallus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bromwich_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bromwich-77">[76]</a></sup> Some linguists have classified emoji and <a href="/wiki/Emoticon" title="Emoticon">emoticons</a> as <a href="/wiki/Discourse_marker" title="Discourse marker">discourse markers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In December 2015 a <a href="/wiki/Sentiment_analysis" title="Sentiment analysis">sentiment analysis</a> of emoji was published,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> and the Emoji Sentiment Ranking 1.0<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> was provided. In 2016, a musical about emoji premiered in Los Angeles.<sup id="cite_ref-playbill.com_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-playbill.com-81">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-timeout.com_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeout.com-82">[81]</a></sup> The computer-animated <i><a href="/wiki/The_Emoji_Movie" title="The Emoji Movie">The Emoji Movie</a></i> was released in summer 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In January 2017, in what is believed to be the first large-scale study of emoji usage, researchers at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a> analysed over 1.2 billion messages input via the Kika Emoji Keyboard<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> and announced that the Face With Tears of Joy was the most popular emoji. The Heart and the Heart eyes emoji stood second and third respectively. The study also found that the French use heart emoji the most.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> People in countries like Australia, France and the Czech Republic used more happy emoji, while this was not so for people in Mexico, Colombia, Chile and Argentina, where people used more negative emoji in comparison to cultural hubs known for restraint and self-discipline, like Turkey, France and Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There has been discussion among legal experts on whether or not emoji could be admissible as evidence in court trials.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> Furthermore, as emoji continue to develop and grow as a "language" of symbols, there may also be the potential of the formation of emoji "dialects".<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> Emoji are being used as more than just to show reactions and emotions.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> Snapchat has even incorporated emoji in its trophy and friends system with each emoji showing a complex meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Emoji_that_further_modern_causes">Emoji that further modern causes</span></h4> 
      <p>On March 5, 2019,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> a drop of blood emoji was released, which is intended to help break the stigma of menstruation.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-94">[93]</a></sup> In addition to normalizing periods, it will also be relevant to describe medical topics such as donating blood and other blood-related activities.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-94">[93]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A mosquito emoji was added in 2018 to raise awareness for diseases spread by the insect, such as dengue and malaria.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emoji_communication_problems"><span id="Emoji_communication_problems">Emoji communication problems</span></span></h2> 
      <p>Research has shown that emoji are often misunderstood. In some cases, this misunderstanding is related to how the actual emoji design is interpreted by the viewer;<sup id="cite_ref-DailyDotMisunderstandEmojis_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DailyDotMisunderstandEmojis-96">[95]</a></sup> in other cases, the emoji that was sent is not shown in the same way on the receiving side.<sup id="cite_ref-GrouplensEmojiMiscommunication_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GrouplensEmojiMiscommunication-97">[96]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The first issue relates to the cultural or contextual interpretation of the emoji. When the author picks an emoji, they think about it in a certain way, but the same character may not trigger the same thoughts in the mind of the receiver<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> (see also <a href="/wiki/Models_of_communication" title="Models of communication">Models of communication</a>). </p>
      <p>For example, people in China have developed a system for using emoji subversively, so that a smiley face could be sent to convey a despising, mocking, and even obnoxious attitude, as the orbicularis oculi (the muscle near that upper eye corner) on the face of the emoji does not move, and the orbicularis oris (the one near the mouth) tightens, which is believed to be a sign of suppressing a smile.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The second problem relates to technology and branding. When an author of a message picks an emoji from a list, it is normally encoded in a non-graphical manner during the transmission, and if the author and the reader do not use the same software or operating system for their devices, the reader's device may visualize the same emoji in a different way. Small changes to a character's look may completely alter its perceived meaning with the receiver. As an example, in April 2020, British actress and presenter <a href="/wiki/Jameela_Jamil" title="Jameela Jamil">Jameela Jamil</a> posted a tweet from her iPhone using the Face with Hand Over Mouth emoji (?) as part of a comment on people shopping for food during the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a>. On Apple's <a href="/wiki/IOS" title="IOS">iOS</a>, the emoji expression is neutral and pensive, but on other platforms the emoji shows as a giggling face. Many fans were initially upset thinking that she, as a well off celebrity, was mocking poor people, but this was not her intended meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversial_emoji">Controversial emoji</span></h3> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">

         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Some emoji have been involved in controversy due to their perceived meanings. Multiple arrests and imprisonments have followed usage of pistol (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F52B</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>), knife (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F5E1</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>), and bomb (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F4A3</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>) emoji in ways that were deemed by authorities to constitute credible threats.<sup id="cite_ref-AppleGun_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AppleGun-101">[100]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the lead-up to the <a href="/wiki/2016_Summer_Olympics" title="2016 Summer Olympics">2016 Summer Olympics</a>, the Unicode Consortium considered proposals to add several Olympic-related emoji, including medals and events such as <a href="/wiki/Handball" title="Handball">handball</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_polo" title="Water polo">water polo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-newsweek_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newsweek-102">[101]</a></sup> By October 2015, these candidate emoji included "<a href="/wiki/ISSF_shooting_events" title="ISSF shooting events">rifle</a>" (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F946</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>) and "<a href="/wiki/Modern_pentathlon" title="Modern pentathlon">modern pentathlon</a>" (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F93B</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-ep20151031_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ep20151031-103">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OlympicRifle_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OlympicRifle-104">[103]</a></sup> However, in 2016, Apple and Microsoft opposed these two emoji, and the characters were added without emoji presentations, meaning that software is expected to render them in black-and-white rather than color, and emoji-specific software such as onscreen keyboards will generally not include them. In addition, while the original incarnations of the modern pentathlon emoji depicted its five events, including a man pointing a gun, the final glyph contains a person riding a horse, along with a laser pistol target in the corner.<sup id="cite_ref-AppleGun_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AppleGun-101">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OlympicRifle_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OlympicRifle-104">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AppleStops_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AppleStops-105">[104]</a></sup> </p> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;max-width:192px">
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:94px;max-width:94px">
          <div class="thumbimage" style="height:92px;overflow:hidden">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Twemoji_1f52b.svg" class="image"><img alt="Drawing of a revolver" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Twemoji_1f52b.svg/92px-Twemoji_1f52b.svg.png" decoding="async" width="92" height="92" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Twemoji_1f52b.svg/138px-Twemoji_1f52b.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Twemoji_1f52b.svg/184px-Twemoji_1f52b.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512"></a>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:94px;max-width:94px">
          <div class="thumbimage" style="height:92px;overflow:hidden">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Twemoji12_1f52b.svg" class="image"><img alt="Drawing of a water pistol" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Twemoji12_1f52b.svg/92px-Twemoji12_1f52b.svg.png" decoding="async" width="92" height="92" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Twemoji12_1f52b.svg/138px-Twemoji12_1f52b.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Twemoji12_1f52b.svg/184px-Twemoji12_1f52b.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512"></a>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="trow" style="display:flex">
         <div class="thumbcaption">
          Original (left) and revised (right) <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> designs, showing the transition from a <a href="/wiki/Revolver" title="Revolver">revolver</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Water_pistol" class="mw-redirect" title="Water pistol">water pistol</a>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On August 1, 2016, Apple announced that in <a href="/wiki/IOS_10" title="IOS 10">iOS 10</a>, the pistol emoji (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F52B</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>) would be changed from a realistic <a href="/wiki/Revolver" title="Revolver">revolver</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Water_pistol" class="mw-redirect" title="Water pistol">water pistol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AppleGun_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AppleGun-101">[100]</a></sup> Conversely, the following day, Microsoft pushed out an update to Windows 10 that changed its longstanding depiction of the pistol emoji as a toy <a href="/wiki/Ray-gun" class="mw-redirect" title="Ray-gun">ray-gun</a> to a real revolver.<sup id="cite_ref-MicrosoftGun_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MicrosoftGun-106">[105]</a></sup> Microsoft stated that the change was made to bring the glyph more in line with industry-standard designs and customer expectations.<sup id="cite_ref-MicrosoftGun_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MicrosoftGun-106">[105]</a></sup> By 2018, most major platforms such as Google, Microsoft, Samsung, Facebook, and Twitter had transitioned their rendering of the pistol emoji to match Apple's water gun implementation.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> Apple's change of depiction from a realistic gun to a toy gun was criticised by among others the editor of <a href="/wiki/Emojipedia" title="Emojipedia">Emojipedia</a>, because it could lead to messages appearing differently to the receiver than the sender had intended.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Insider_Inc." title="Insider Inc.">Insider</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> Rob Price said it created the potential for "serious miscommunication across different platforms", and asked "What if a joke sent from an Apple user to a Google user is misconstrued because of differences in rendering? Or if a genuine threat sent by a Google user to an Apple user goes unreported because it is taken as a joke?"<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Eggplant" title="Eggplant">eggplant</a> (British English: <span lang="en">aubergine</span>) emoji (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F346</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>) has also seen controversy due to it being used, almost solely<sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (July 2020)">better&nbsp;source&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> in North America, to represent a penis.<sup id="cite_ref-AmericanDialect_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmericanDialect-75">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bromwich_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bromwich-77">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EggplantRising_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EggplantRising-110">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SordidHistory_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SordidHistory-111">[110]</a></sup> Beginning in December 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Hashtag" title="Hashtag">hashtag</a> <span class="nowrap">#EggplantFridays</span> began to rise to popularity on <a href="/wiki/Instagram" title="Instagram">Instagram</a> for use in marking photos featuring clothed or unclothed penises.<sup id="cite_ref-EggplantRising_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EggplantRising-110">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SordidHistory_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SordidHistory-111">[110]</a></sup> This became such a popular trend that beginning in April 2015, Instagram disabled the ability to search for not only the <span class="nowrap">#EggplantFridays</span> tag, but also other eggplant-containing hashtags, including simply <span class="nowrap">#eggplant</span> and <span class="nowrap">#?</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-EggplantRising_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EggplantRising-110">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SordidHistory_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SordidHistory-111">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-InstagramBlocks_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InstagramBlocks-112">[111]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Peach" title="Peach">peach</a> emoji (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F351</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>) has likewise been used as a euphemistic icon for <a href="/wiki/Buttocks" title="Buttocks">buttocks</a>, with a 2016 <a href="/wiki/Emojipedia" title="Emojipedia">Emojipedia</a> analysis revealing that only 7% of English language tweets with the peach emoji refer to the actual fruit.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> In 2016, Apple attempted to redesign the emoji to less resemble buttocks. This was met with fierce backlash in beta testing and Apple reversed its decision by the time it went live to the public.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In December 2017, a lawyer in <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a>, India, threatened to file suit against <a href="/wiki/WhatsApp" title="WhatsApp">WhatsApp</a> for allowing use of the <a href="/wiki/The_finger" title="The finger">middle finger</a> emoji (<span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+1F595</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;"></span>) on the basis that the company is "directly abetting the use of an offensive, lewd, obscene gesture" in violation of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Penal_Code" title="Indian Penal Code">Indian Penal Code</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emoji_versus_text_presentation">Emoji versus text presentation</span></h2> 
      <p>Unicode defines <a href="/wiki/Variant_form_(Unicode)" title="Variant form (Unicode)">variation sequences</a> for many of its emoji to indicate their desired presentation. </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>Emoji characters can have two main kinds of presentation: </p>
       <ul>
        <li>an <i>emoji presentation</i>, with colorful and perhaps whimsical shapes, even animated</li> 
        <li>a <i>text presentation</i>, such as black &amp; white
         <div class="templatequotecite">
          —?<cite>Unicode Technical Report #51: Unicode Emoji<sup id="cite_ref-UTR51_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UTR51-54">[54]</a></sup></cite>
         </div></li>
       </ul>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Specifying the desired presentation is done by following the base emoji with either U+FE0E VARIATION SELECTOR-15 (VS15) for text or U+FE0F VARIATION SELECTOR-16 (VS16) for emoji-style.<sup id="cite_ref-UniStdVarTxt_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UniStdVarTxt-118">[117]</a></sup> </p> 
      <table class="wikitable nounderlines" style="border-collapse:collapse;background:#FFFFFF;font-size:150%;text-align:center">

       </caption>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Skin_color">Skin color</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Emoji_modifiers" class="mw-redirect" title="Emoji modifiers">Emoji modifiers</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Five symbol modifier characters were added with Unicode 8.0 to provide a range of skin tones for human emoji. These modifiers are called EMOJI MODIFIER FITZPATRICK TYPE-1-2, -3, -4, -5, and -6 (U+1F3FB–U+1F3FF): ? ? ? ? ?. They are based on the <a href="/wiki/Fitzpatrick_scale" title="Fitzpatrick scale">Fitzpatrick scale</a> for classifying human skin color. Human emoji that are not followed by one of these five modifiers should be displayed in a generic, non-realistic skin tone, such as bright yellow (<span style="color: #ffcc22;font-size:large;">?</span>), blue (<span style="color: #3399CC;font-size:large;">?</span>), or gray (<span style="color: #CCCCCC;font-size:large;">?</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-UTR51_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UTR51-54">[54]</a></sup> Non-human emoji (like <span class="nowrap">
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"><span class="monospaced">U+26FD</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">?</span> <span style="font-size:85%;">FUEL PUMP</span>) are unaffected by the Fitzpatrick modifiers. As of Unicode 13.0, Fitzpatrick modifiers can be used with 118 human emoji spread across six blocks: <a href="/wiki/Dingbat#Skin_tones" title="Dingbat">Dingbats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emoticons_(Unicode_block)#Skin_tones" title="Emoticons (Unicode block)">Emoticons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miscellaneous_Symbols#Skin_tones" title="Miscellaneous Symbols">Miscellaneous Symbols</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miscellaneous_Symbols_and_Pictographs#Skin_tones" title="Miscellaneous Symbols and Pictographs">Miscellaneous Symbols and Pictographs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supplemental_Symbols_and_Pictographs#Skin_tones" title="Supplemental Symbols and Pictographs">Supplemental Symbols and Pictographs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Transport_and_Map_Symbols#Skin_tones" title="Transport and Map Symbols">Transport and Map Symbols</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EmojiData_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EmojiData-12">[12]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The following table shows both the Unicode characters and the open-source "Twemoji" images, designed by <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a>: </p>

         <td><a href="/wiki/File:Twemoji2_1f469-1f3ff.svg" class="image"><img alt="Twemoji2 1f469-1f3ff.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Twemoji2_1f469-1f3ff.svg/30px-Twemoji2_1f469-1f3ff.svg.png" decoding="async" width="30" height="30" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Twemoji2_1f469-1f3ff.svg/45px-Twemoji2_1f469-1f3ff.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Twemoji2_1f469-1f3ff.svg/60px-Twemoji2_1f469-1f3ff.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512"></a> </td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Joining">Joining</span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Zero_Width_(Non)Joiner.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Zero_Width_%28Non%29Joiner.svg/220px-Zero_Width_%28Non%29Joiner.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="47" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Zero_Width_%28Non%29Joiner.svg/330px-Zero_Width_%28Non%29Joiner.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Zero_Width_%28Non%29Joiner.svg/440px-Zero_Width_%28Non%29Joiner.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1113" data-file-height="237"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Zero_Width_(Non)Joiner.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Behaviour of the 
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r936282034"><a href="/wiki/Zero-width_joiner" title="Zero-width joiner">ZWJ</a> and 
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r936282034"><a href="/wiki/Zero-width_non-joiner" title="Zero-width non-joiner">ZWNJ</a> format controls with various types of character, including emoji.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Implementations may use <a href="/wiki/Zero-width_joiner" title="Zero-width joiner">U+200D ZERO WIDTH JOINER</a> (ZWJ) between emoji to make them behave like a single, unique emoji character.<sup id="cite_ref-UTR51_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UTR51-54">[54]</a></sup> (Systems that don't support this should ignore the ZWJ character.) </p>
      <p>For example, the sequence U+1F468 MAN, U+200D ZWJ, U+1F469 WOMAN, U+200D ZWJ, U+1F467 GIRL (?????) could be displayed as a single emoji depicting a family with a man, a woman, and a girl if the implementation supports it. Systems that don't support it would ignore the ZWJs, showing the base emoji in the sequence: U+1F468 MAN, U+1F469 WOMAN, U+1F467 GIRL (???). </p>
      <p><span class="anchor" id="RGI"></span>Unicode previously maintained a catalog of emoji ZWJ sequences that were supported on at least one commonly available platform. The consortium has since switched to documenting sequences that are <i>recommended for general interchange</i> (RGI). These are clusters that emoji fonts are expected to include as part of the standard.<sup id="cite_ref-ZWJSequences_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ZWJSequences-14">[14]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Unicode_blocks">Unicode blocks</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Dingbat#Dingbats_Unicode_block" title="Dingbat">Dingbats (Unicode block)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emoticons_(Unicode_block)" title="Emoticons (Unicode block)">Emoticons (Unicode block)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miscellaneous_Symbols" title="Miscellaneous Symbols">Miscellaneous Symbols (Unicode block)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miscellaneous_Symbols_and_Pictographs" title="Miscellaneous Symbols and Pictographs">Miscellaneous Symbols and Pictographs (Unicode block)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supplemental_Symbols_and_Pictographs" title="Supplemental Symbols and Pictographs">Supplemental Symbols and Pictographs (Unicode block)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Symbols_and_Pictographs_Extended-A" title="Symbols and Pictographs Extended-A">Symbols and Pictographs Extended-A (Unicode block)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Transport_and_Map_Symbols" title="Transport and Map Symbols">Transport and Map Symbols (Unicode block)</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Unicode 13.0 represents emoji using 1,367 characters spread across 24 blocks, of which 26 are <a href="/wiki/Regional_Indicator_Symbol" class="mw-redirect" title="Regional Indicator Symbol">Regional Indicator Symbols</a> that combine in pairs to form flag emoji, and 12 (#, * and 0–9) are base characters for <a href="/wiki/Keycap" title="Keycap">keycap</a> emoji sequences:<sup id="cite_ref-EmojiData_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EmojiData-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UTR51_54-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UTR51-54">[54]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>637 of the 768 code points in the <a href="/wiki/Miscellaneous_Symbols_and_Pictographs" title="Miscellaneous Symbols and Pictographs">Miscellaneous Symbols and Pictographs</a> block are considered emoji. 240 of the 254 code points in the <a href="/wiki/Supplemental_Symbols_and_Pictographs" title="Supplemental Symbols and Pictographs">Supplemental Symbols and Pictographs</a> block are considered emoji. All of the 57 code points in the <a href="/wiki/Symbols_and_Pictographs_Extended-A" title="Symbols and Pictographs Extended-A">Symbols and Pictographs Extended-A</a> block are considered emoji. All of the 80 code points in the <a href="/wiki/Emoticons" class="mw-redirect" title="Emoticons">Emoticons</a> block are considered emoji. 101 of the 114 code points in the <a href="/wiki/Transport_and_Map_Symbols" title="Transport and Map Symbols">Transport and Map Symbols</a> block are considered emoji. 83 of the 256 code points in the <a href="/wiki/Miscellaneous_Symbols" title="Miscellaneous Symbols">Miscellaneous Symbols</a> block are considered emoji. 33 of the 192 code points in the <a href="/wiki/Dingbat#Unicode" title="Dingbat">Dingbats</a> block are considered emoji. </p> 
      <table class="wikitable nounderlines" style="border-collapse:collapse;background:#FFFFFF;font-size:large;font-family:'Apple Color Emoji','Segoe UI Emoji','Segoe UI Symbol','Noto Color Emoji';text-align:center">

            4.<span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_U1F602_data"><b><a href="#ref_U1F602_data">^</a></b></span> 
            <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://unicode.org/Public/UNIDATA/emoji/emoji-data.txt">"UCD: Emoji Data for UTR #51"</a>.
